    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }

      AppleID.auth.init({

<span id="freeTextContainer8005720953448833966">Dive into this classic from the singular mind of Edgar Allan Poe, who is widely regarded as the master of short horror fiction. "The Fall of the House of Usher" recounts the terrible events that befall the last remaining members of the once-illustrious Usher clan before it is -- quite literally -- rent asunder. With amazing economy, Poe plunges the reader into a state of d</span>
  <span id="freeText8005720953448833966" style="display:none">Dive into this classic from the singular mind of Edgar Allan Poe, who is widely regarded as the master of short horror fiction. "The Fall of the House of Usher" recounts the terrible events that befall the last remaining members of the once-illustrious Usher clan before it is -- quite literally -- rent asunder. With amazing economy, Poe plunges the reader into a state of deliciously agonizing suspense. It's a must-read for fans of the golden era of horror writing.</span>

<span class='communityAnswerText'>
<span class="expandableContainer"><span class="truncatedContent">This story made you think outside the box. The way he described objects and made them have feelings were deep. The way he describes the sister is even<a class="jsShow showAction" href="#">…more</a></span><span class="fullContent">This story made you think outside the box. The way he described objects and made them have feelings were deep. The way he describes the sister is even better. But the part that got me was when he was reading the book during the storm and then here comes the sounds. How Roderick was ready for what was coming. And then he left like he came. <a class="jsHide hideAction" href="#">(less)</a></span></span>

        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1284305462?book_show_action=true">May 19, 2015</a>

        <a title="Jeffrey Keeten" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jeffrey Keeten" href="/user/show/3427339-jeffrey-keeten">Jeffrey Keeten</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3427339-jeffrey-keeten?shelf=gothic">gothic</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3427339-jeffrey-keeten?shelf=horror">horror</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1284305462" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5935787768296871648"><b>"There was an iciness, a sinking, a sickening of the heart—an unredeemed dreariness of thought which no goading of the imagination could torture into aught of the sublime. What was it—I paused to think—what was it that so unnerved me in the contemplation of the House of Usher?"</b><br/><br/><a href='https://s1183.photobucket.com/user/jkeeten/media/House20of20Usher_zpscczhmftg.jpg.html' target='_blank' rel='nofollow noopener'>
  <img src='https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1532607679i/26031431.jpg' alt=' photo House20of20Usher_zpscczhmftg.jpg' class='gr-hostedUserImg'/>
</a><br/><b>A gothic house that instantly made me think of the House of Usher.</b><br/><br/>When our narrator has been summoned to the bedside of his sick friend Roderick Usher, he finds a household overcast with gloom. If an environment can pe</span>
  <span id="freeText5935787768296871648" style="display:none"><b>"There was an iciness, a sinking, a sickening of the heart—an unredeemed dreariness of thought which no goading of the imagination could torture into aught of the sublime. What was it—I paused to think—what was it that so unnerved me in the contemplation of the House of Usher?"</b><br><br><a href="https://s1183.photobucket.com/user/jkeeten/media/House20of20Usher_zpscczhmftg.jpg.html" target="_blank" rel="nofollow noopener">
  <img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1532607679i/26031431.jpg" alt=" photo House20of20Usher_zpscczhmftg.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg">
</a><br><b>A gothic house that instantly made me think of the House of Usher.</b><br><br>When our narrator has been summoned to the bedside of his sick friend Roderick Usher, he finds a household overcast with gloom. If an environment can permeate a soul with melancholy and fear, then the House of Usher is a detriment to all who enter. Our narrator begins to feel the effects almost immediately. <i>"I felt that I breathed an atmosphere of sorrow. An air of stern, deep, and irredeemable gloom hung over and pervaded all."</i><br><br>Roderick is suffering from numerous illnesses, all undiagnosable in the 1800s. This story predates the modern psychology that eventually is able to put a name to those illnesses: hyperesthesia, hypochondria, and severe anxiety. This trilogy of maladies can start to erode the ability of the mind to reason. His twin sister Madeline is also sick and is frequently discovered sleepwalking or really something more like death walking. <br><br>The atmosphere is beginning to wear on our narrator as well. He likes Roderick and enjoys composing songs, writing poetry, and painting pictures with him, but even as they manage to ignore the malaise of their circumstances for a few hours, the melancholy is always lurking to reassert itself on their senses. <b>"An atmosphere which had no affinity with the air of heaven, but which had reeked up from the decayed trees, and the gray wall, and the silent tarn—a pestilent and mystic vapor, dull, sluggish, faintly discernible, and leaden-hued."</b> He begins to feel uneasy all the time and is beginning to believe that Roderick is not afraid of a sickness producing paranormal, but is actually, justifiably afraid of something real, but unknowable. <br><br>Our imaginations can always conjure up worse horrors than those we can actually see. <br><br><a href="https://s1183.photobucket.com/user/jkeeten/media/Usher_zpsxwycoraw.jpg.html" target="_blank" rel="nofollow noopener">
  <img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1532607679i/26031432._SY540_.jpg" alt=" photo Usher_zpsxwycoraw.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg">
</a><b>Illustration by Harry Clarke whose work is often mistakenly attributed to Aubrey Beardsley</b><br><br>When Madeline dies, things begin to unravel. Our narrator finds himself helping Roderick to take her down in the family tombs. Madeline appears more alive in death than she did in life. Her cheeks are even rosy. Roderick insists that they screw down the coffin lid.<br><br>Let’s just say the story ends with a bang. <br><br>I recently started reading the Robert McCammon book Usher’s Passing and realized that it has been a long time since I’d read the Edgar Allan Poe story that was the inspiration for that novel. I’ve always enjoyed the ripe symbolism that is always a characteristic of a good Poe story. The reader experiences this growing uneasiness as the story unspools. Poe seemingly effortlessly conveys this sense of impending doom. When I was breaking sentences down to see how Poe was doing this, I realized that it wasn’t effortless, but masterful. <br><br><a href="https://s1183.photobucket.com/user/jkeeten/media/Usher20Poe_zpsmxovy2cj.jpg.html" target="_blank" rel="nofollow noopener">
  <img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1532607679i/26031433._SY540_.jpg" alt=" photo Usher20Poe_zpsmxovy2cj.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg">
</a><br><b>Another awesome illustration from Harry Clarke</b><br><br>I liked Poe even before I discovered that I shared a birthday (January 19th) with him. He was appreciated in his time more by the French than he was by the Americans. I’ve seen it mentioned several times where American travelers to Europe expressed their bafflement at being asked about this American writer who they had never heard of. On some late night, when you are having trouble sleeping, read a story or two of Poe and notice the psychological impact he starts to have on you as your eyes dart around the room at what sounded like a creaking floorboard or your skin crawls at the screech of an owl that may have been the last scream of a woman ensnared. <br><br>If you wish to see more of my most recent book and movie reviews, visit <a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="http://www.jeffreykeeten.com">http://www.jeffreykeeten.com</a><br>I also have a Facebook blogger page at: <a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="https://www.facebook.com/JeffreyKeeten">https://www.facebook.com/JeffreyKeeten</a></span>
  <a data-text-id="5935787768296871648" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/16254355-matthew?shelf=2020">2020</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/16254355-matthew?shelf=horror">horror</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/16254355-matthew?shelf=audio">audio</a>,

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3269514134" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2268773283774966247">Perhaps the perfect story for the COVID-19 quarantine! I say this for two reasons.<br/><br/>First: The length<br/><br/>This story can be consumed in one sitting. At a time when I am struggling to focus on reading, getting in and getting out of a story quickly is perfect for my current attention span. Also, I decided to listen to this one. As part of keeping myself healthy during this time of isolation at home, I have been trying to get some fresh air by going for a walk at lunch. I was able to start and finish this</span>
  <span id="freeText2268773283774966247" style="display:none">Perhaps the perfect story for the COVID-19 quarantine! I say this for two reasons.<br /><br />First: The length<br /><br />This story can be consumed in one sitting. At a time when I am struggling to focus on reading, getting in and getting out of a story quickly is perfect for my current attention span. Also, I decided to listen to this one. As part of keeping myself healthy during this time of isolation at home, I have been trying to get some fresh air by going for a walk at lunch. I was able to start and finish this entire story over the course of one walk. It felt like an accomplishment, and I needed that for my reading morale.<br /><br />Second: The plot<br /><br />The story of this book focuses on a man who has been in isolation for an extended period of time and is deteriorating into madness. The house in the story represents the stability, <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[ and eventual collapse <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span>, of his mind. As we are all stuck at home and feeling the mental strain that can put on us, it is very easy to relate to what might happen to your brain after being stuck in the same house day after day after day after day.<br /><br />Definitely recommend! But, if you are struggling with the isolation of quarantine at the moment and you are looking to read something that does not remind you of it, this might be worth staying away from for a while.<br /></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1982076452" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6380443674267270800">Wow, what a fantastic story. You have all the gothic elements crammed in here: a haunted (perhaps even sentient) house, a mysterious illness, madness, death, entombment, a dungeon, a violent storm, a cursed family, hints of possible incest (?), resurrection, bizarre poetry, and a story-within-the-story about a knight slaying a dragon. And binding this all together is Poe's inimitable style and narrative drive. It's horror of the creepy, atmospheric kind (the best kind, IMHO), the kind that gets </span>
  <span id="freeText6380443674267270800" style="display:none">Wow, what a fantastic story. You have all the gothic elements crammed in here: a haunted (perhaps even sentient) house, a mysterious illness, madness, death, entombment, a dungeon, a violent storm, a cursed family, hints of possible incest (?), resurrection, bizarre poetry, and a story-within-the-story about a knight slaying a dragon. And binding this all together is Poe's inimitable style and narrative drive. It's horror of the creepy, atmospheric kind (the best kind, IMHO), the kind that gets under your skin and makes you feel it in a thousand subtle ways.</span>

<div id="review_2169652756" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2169652756" />
    <a title="Tadiana ✩Night Owl☽" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/8734459-tadiana-night-owl"><img alt="Tadiana ✩Night Owl☽" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1603085284p2/8734459.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2169652756?book_show_action=true">Oct 31, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Tadiana ✩Night Owl☽" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Tadiana ✩Night Owl☽" href="/user/show/8734459-tadiana-night-owl">Tadiana ✩Night Owl☽</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/8734459-tadiana-night-owl?shelf=classics">classics</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/8734459-tadiana-night-owl?shelf=horror">horror</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/8734459-tadiana-night-owl?shelf=the-shorts">the-shorts</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/8734459-tadiana-night-owl?shelf=gothic">gothic</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2169652756" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer530391083395347116">Edgar Allan Poe's "The Fall of the House of Usher" is one of the original haunted house tales. This story embodies old-fashioned gothic horror.<br/><br/><img src='https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1509381113i/24314846._SY540_.jpg' width='290' height='365' alt='description' class='gr-hostedUserImg'/> <br/><i>Arthur Rackham illustration</i><br/><br/>The unnamed narrator tells of his visit to the dreary country home of his boyhood friend, Roderick Usher. He notices (and describes at length) how both Roderick and his house are crumbling at the edges. Roderick is a deeply mentally disturbed person; his sister Madeline, who wafts past the two men once without regarding them, s</span>
  <span id="freeText530391083395347116" style="display:none">Edgar Allan Poe's "The Fall of the House of Usher" is one of the original haunted house tales. This story embodies old-fashioned gothic horror.<br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1509381113i/24314846._SY540_.jpg" width="290" height="365" alt="description" class="gr-hostedUserImg"> <br><i>Arthur Rackham illustration</i><br><br>The unnamed narrator tells of his visit to the dreary country home of his boyhood friend, Roderick Usher. He notices (and describes at length) how both Roderick and his house are crumbling at the edges. Roderick is a deeply mentally disturbed person; his sister Madeline, who wafts past the two men once without regarding them, seems equally troubled, but in different ways. And there's something unexpressed but troubling about the relationship between brother and sister.*<br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1509492590i/24327356._SY540_.jpg" width="400" height="600" alt="description" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><i>1919 illustration for this story by Harry Clarke. It doesn't seem to track the story exactly, but it's certainly a weirdly marvelous drawing</i><br><br>This story struck me at first as too verbose - Poe gets perhaps a bit carried away with his descriptions of decay, both in the narrator's friend, Roderick Usher, and in his sister (who at different times reminded me of a ghost or a vampire), and in their house itself. But things get creepier as the story moves along, and the ending is truly chilling.<br><br>The physical house of the Ushers, with its large crack in its walls, and its decrepitude and instability, is mirrored in the persons of Roderick Usher and his twin sister. "House of Usher," of course, can mean either the physical house or the family dynasty, a point Poe makes expressly clear. "Usher," too, reverbates with meaning: what kind of a godforsaken place is the narrator - and we as readers - being ushered into?<br><br>*<a href="http://www.sparknotes.com/lit/poestories/section3.rhtml" rel="nofollow noopener">SparkNotes</a> offers this opinion: "The family has no enduring branches, so all genetic transmission has occurred incestuously within the domain of the house." Ewww!<br><br>Free online many places, including <a href="http://xroads.virginia.edu/~hyper/poe/fall.html" rel="nofollow noopener">here</a>.</span>
  <a data-text-id="530391083395347116" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

Edgar Allan Poe's The Fall of the House of Usher is a nearly perfect short story. It creates tension as events unfold and the once familiar takes on the visage of the ghastly and wild. Poe successfully evokes a feeling of dread which is inescapable. And while there is closure in the story, the narrator is unnerved by the sorrow and recognizes that he will carry the terror the r</span>
  <span id="freeText10365278472932412847" style="display:none">Along with the unnamed narrator, we as readers are summoned into the macabre to witness the fall of the House of Usher. Edgar Allan Poe's The Fall of the House of Usher is a nearly perfect short story. It creates tension as events unfold and the once familiar takes on the visage of the ghastly and wild. Poe successfully evokes a feeling of dread which is inescapable. And while there is closure in the story, the narrator is unnerved by the sorrow and recognizes that he will carry the terror the rest of his life. We too are touched by this terror in ways that are impossible to fathom. Great story! </span>

<div id="review_1749144785" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">

        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1749144785?book_show_action=true">Sep 05, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="James" class="user" itemprop="url" name="James" href="/user/show/7365405-james">James</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7365405-james?shelf=1-fiction">1-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7365405-james?shelf=4-written-pre-20th-century">4-written-pre-20th-century</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1749144785" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11007817016859673555">3.5 of 5 stars to <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/175516.The_Fall_of_the_House_of_Usher' title='The Fall of the House of Usher by Edgar Allan Poe' rel='nofollow noopener'>The Fall of the House of Usher</a>, a short story by <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/4624490.Edgar_Allan_Poe' title='Edgar Allan Poe' rel='nofollow noopener'>Edgar Allan Poe</a>, written in 1839. I found myself a slight bit bored the first time I read it. It seemed to only be about some guy that went to go visit an old school buddy. When he arrives, some type of curse or disastrous mood hangs over his house and looms until the man is a bit fearful. Then, his best friend is dying of some odd disease. They watch his wife die, but only when the man is about to die himself does he reveal that</span>
  <span id="freeText11007817016859673555" style="display:none">3.5 of 5 stars to <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/175516.The_Fall_of_the_House_of_Usher" title="The Fall of the House of Usher by Edgar Allan Poe" rel="nofollow noopener">The Fall of the House of Usher</a>, a short story by <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/4624490.Edgar_Allan_Poe" title="Edgar Allan Poe" rel="nofollow noopener">Edgar Allan Poe</a>, written in 1839. I found myself a slight bit bored the first time I read it. It seemed to only be about some guy that went to go visit an old school buddy. When he arrives, some type of curse or disastrous mood hangs over his house and looms until the man is a bit fearful. Then, his best friend is dying of some odd disease. They watch his wife die, but only when the man is about to die himself does he reveal that he buried the woman alive. She is still down there breathing. It was powerful imagery of the heart still beating and her breaths. It was unlike in “The Tell-Tale Heart” when the heart wasn’t really beating, a figment of his imagination. This time, it was real. Fast forward a few years later, I read the story again at the end of my college years, as a look on mystery and the Gothic origins. And the story is really vivid. It's not Poe's best, but you really get a sense of his imagery and his talent for describing things in a most unique way.<br /><br /><b>
  <i>

And you can find all my social media profiles to get the details on the who/what/when/where and my pictures. </span>

<div id="review_755098213" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/755098213" />
    <a title="Bionic Jean" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/19300421-bionic-jean"><img alt="Bionic Jean" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1586709362p2/19300421.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/755098213?book_show_action=true">Nov 02, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Bionic Jean" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Bionic Jean" href="/user/show/19300421-bionic-jean">Bionic Jean</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Kindle Edition " href="/book/show/6234638-the-fall-of-the-house-of-usher">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/19300421-bionic-jean?shelf=classics">classics</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/19300421-bionic-jean?shelf=read-authors-m-p">read-authors-m-p</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/19300421-bionic-jean?shelf=ghost-horror-supernatural">ghost-horror-supernatural</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/19300421-bionic-jean?shelf=19th-century-ish">19th-century-ish</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer755098213" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9343648932821371665"><b>The Fall of the House of Usher </b> (published in 1839) may well be one of the stories which started the current interest in the gothic genre, although Ann Radcliffe's <i>"The Mysteries of Udolpho"</i>, for instance, had been published much earlier in 1794. Apart from its parody in Jane Austen's <i> "Northanger Abbey"</i>, Radcliffe's work has now largely been forgotten along with other great gothic works from the time. Yet <b>The Fall of the House of Usher </b> remains perennially popular and influential. Poe regarded </span>
  <span id="freeText9343648932821371665" style="display:none"><b>The Fall of the House of Usher </b> (published in 1839) may well be one of the stories which started the current interest in the gothic genre, although Ann Radcliffe's <i>"The Mysteries of Udolpho"</i>, for instance, had been published much earlier in 1794. Apart from its parody in Jane Austen's <i> "Northanger Abbey"</i>, Radcliffe's work has now largely been forgotten along with other great gothic works from the time. Yet <b>The Fall of the House of Usher </b> remains perennially popular and influential. Poe regarded it as his most successful example of <i>"totality" </i>, in that every detail and event in the story is relevant to the plot.<br /><br />The viewpoint character has been invited to the house of a childhood friend, Roderick Usher, in order to cheer him as he is weak, ill and depressed. <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[ Once there, Usher is found to be a hypochondriac, suffering greatly with nervous agitation. He and his sister Madeline, who also has the similar ghastly affliction, are the last of the line.  <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span><br /><br />Very early on in this story we are encouraged to empathise with the narrator, as his surroundings become increasingly grotesque, sinister and threatening. The <i>"House of Usher"</i>, we are told, describes both the family and the mansion itself, and on learning this snippet of information the ending to this story is neatly telegraphed, albeit on an almost subconscious level.<br /><br />Poe is at the height of his powers of description in this tale. Here is the man's first sight of the house:<br /><br /><i>"about the whole mansion and domain there hung an atmosphere peculiar to themselves and their immediate vicinity which had no affinity with the air of heaven, but which had reeked up from the decayed trees, and the gray wall, and the silent tarn - a pestilent and mystic vapor, dull, sluggish, faintly discernable, and leaden-hued."</i><br /><br />And here's another atmospheric depiction, of his room this time:<br /><br /><i>"the bewildering influence of the gloomy furniture of the room - of the dark and tattered draperies which, tortured into motion by the breath of a rising tempest, swayed fitfully to and fro upon the walls, and rustled uneasily about the decorations about my bed."</i><br /><br />Or what about this evocative description of (super)natural phenomena: <br /><br /><i>"the under surfaces of the huge masses of agitated vapor, as well as all terrestrial objects immediately around us, were glowing in the unnatural light of a faintly luminous and distinctly visible gaseous exhalation which hung about and enshrouded the mansion." </i><br /><br />The whole tale is superbly imbued with a sense of foreboding and impending doom. Conversation is virtually absent; the only occasions being for dramatic effect, for example <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[ near the end when Usher bursts forth with an impassioned speech, <br /><br /><i>"Oh pity me, miserable wretch that I am - I dare not - I dare not speak. We have put her living in the tomb!" </i><br /><br />Indeed this speech goes on so long that the reader is thrilled by a suspicion that Usher is now completely insane and gabbling nonsense. <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span><br /><br />The culmination of this story is a masterpiece of gothic description. Our credulity is stretched as the characters reach a point of hysteria, <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[ and the surroundings themselves become increasingly sentient. The inkling about the ending which was dangled intriguingly before the reader at the start is satisfyingly proved correct. Both family and house are by then intertwined in an almost organic sense, and their demise is powerful and surreal. Was it wholly due to a tornado, <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span> or was something more supernatural at work?</span>
  <a data-text-id="9343648932821371665" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2283921555" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2283921555" />
    <a title="Tom Lewis" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/41086818-tom-lewis"><img alt="Tom Lewis" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1428092317p2/41086818.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2283921555?book_show_action=true">Dec 28, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Tom Lewis" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Tom Lewis" href="/user/show/41086818-tom-lewis">Tom Lewis</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2283921555" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer429400729656105328">One of the creepiest, eeriest haunted house stories I’ve ever read. From the first few lines, a disquieting sense a dread begins to build, and it never lets up. The story has the narrator being summoned to a remote decaying mansion where his childhood friend – the last of a great dynasty, is near death. And just wait until something stalks the mansion’s dark halls in the dead of night. Like HP Lovecraft, Poe’s choice of words brilliantly teases the imagination with its dark terrors. </span>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1449120184" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1449120184" />
    <a title="kohey" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/8936504-kohey"><img alt="kohey" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1497769102p2/8936504.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1449120184?book_show_action=true">Nov 22, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="kohey" class="user" itemprop="url" name="kohey" href="/user/show/8936504-kohey">kohey</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Kindle Edition " href="/book/show/18889045-the-fall-of-the-house-of-usher">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1449120184" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7929761020706281583">This dark,somber and melancholic world makes my head spinning.</span>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_1918034876" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1918034876" />
    <a title="Meike" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/59050228-meike"><img alt="Meike" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1514995700p2/59050228.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1918034876?book_show_action=true">Feb 19, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Meike" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Meike" href="/user/show/59050228-meike">Meike</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Kindle Edition " href="/book/show/53719884-the-fall-of-the-house-of-usher">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/59050228-meike?shelf=usa">usa</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/59050228-meike?shelf=2020-read">2020-read</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1918034876" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12203288263835403144">Aaaahhhhh, gothic psychological horror extraordinaire - who can resist the sinister appeal of Eddie Poe's twisted tales? Here, an enigmatic narrator (who is this unnamed dude?) receives a letter from his childhood friend Roderick Usher who begs him to come visit. The narrator obliges and rides to the crumbling mansion where Roddy and his twin sister Madeline reside. The noble members of the dynastic house of Usher have long suffered from nervous conditions and acute sensibilities, and frail Rodd</span>
  <span id="freeText12203288263835403144" style="display:none">Aaaahhhhh, gothic psychological horror extraordinaire - who can resist the sinister appeal of Eddie Poe's twisted tales? Here, an enigmatic narrator (who is this unnamed dude?) receives a letter from his childhood friend Roderick Usher who begs him to come visit. The narrator obliges and rides to the crumbling mansion where Roddy and his twin sister Madeline reside. The noble members of the dynastic house of Usher have long suffered from nervous conditions and acute sensibilities, and frail Roddy informs his guest that his sister his about to die - which promptly happens...maybe. During a stormy night, Roddy confesses that he has buried his sister alive..but did he really?<br /><br />Once again, Poe investigates the malfunctions of the human mind. The crumbling mansion corresponds with Roderick's disintegrating mind, his twin sister mirros him like the mansion is mirrored in the lake that will finally swallow the house and, with it, the last two members of the Usher family. Is the relationship between Roderick and Medeline incestous? (Remember that Poe married his underage cousin!) Are these pale, frail creatures vampires? Does the wheather outside influence what happens in the house or is it in fact the other way around?<br /><br />As we do not know much about the narrator except the nature of his relationship to crazy Roddy, we don't know what might affect his perceptions - and on top of that, there is a lot of background information that he himself obviously doesn't have but that would be crucial to make sense of what happens in the House of Usher. As a consequence, we as readers start speculating and connecting the dots - and as there a re multiple interpretations that make sense, this story can be read again and again without losing its spooky appeal. Lovers of the arts must also appreciate how sneaky Poe insinuates that mentally deranged people tend to be great artists, Roderick not being the only example in the Usher family. <br /><br />Super elegant, super smart, super spooky - Poe still rules Halloween. <br />Btw: Check out the music video to <a href="https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=uoZ4zWuu2Do" rel="nofollow noopener">"Blue Orchid"</a> by The White Stripes and enjoy the House of Usher- vibes!</span>
  <a data-text-id="12203288263835403144" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1930617755" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1930617755" />
    <a title="David Schaafsma" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/269235-david-schaafsma"><img alt="David Schaafsma" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1574997804p2/269235.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1930617755?book_show_action=true">Mar 05, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="David Schaafsma" class="user" itemprop="url" name="David Schaafsma" href="/user/show/269235-david-schaafsma">David Schaafsma</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/269235-david-schaafsma?shelf=short-stories">short-stories</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/269235-david-schaafsma?shelf=fiction-19th-century">fiction-19th-century</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/269235-david-schaafsma?shelf=horror">horror</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/269235-david-schaafsma?shelf=eng-240-spr-17">eng-240-spr-17</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1930617755" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5206957705232947831">“The Fall of the House of Usher” is a classic Poe story, and helps us define just what it means to be “gothic” in nineteenth century literature. Continuous dark, stormy weather, a huge decaying gothic-style house, continuous pervasive gloom, humans infused with all this.<br/><br/>Roderick Usher, pale and wild-haired owner of the house. Sick, maybe from the waters seeping from the tarn into the house? The fungi on the building? Is Roderick an opium eater? Living with his also pale and wild-haired wraith tw</span>
  <span id="freeText5206957705232947831" style="display:none">“The Fall of the House of Usher” is a classic Poe story, and helps us define just what it means to be “gothic” in nineteenth century literature. Continuous dark, stormy weather, a huge decaying gothic-style house, continuous pervasive gloom, humans infused with all this.<br /><br />Roderick Usher, pale and wild-haired owner of the house. Sick, maybe from the waters seeping from the tarn into the house? The fungi on the building? Is Roderick an opium eater? Living with his also pale and wild-haired wraith twin sister Madeline. Mental disorder, nervous agitation, mysterious house. Lead poisoning? (imposing a contemporary theory. . .). House decayed, with a crack in the foundation, tall ceilings, dimly lit.<br /><br />Sonorous, formal language on the verge of the ridiculous: “cadaverousness, “ “pertinacity,” “phantasmagoric.” Language that matches the house, a little stuffy.<br /><br />“An excited and highly distempered ideality.” A romantic vision filled with dread, fear. <br /><br />[“I am interested in language because it wounds or seduces me.” –Barthes]<br /> <br />The pleasures of the text, satisfying or at least calling up desire: aching, seductive. Tacking between the laughter of desire and the tears of heartbreak, loss. Death and darkness as delicious pleasures. <br /><br />[Text of bliss: the text that imposes a state of loss, the text that discomforts (perhaps to the point of a certain boredom), unsettles the reader's historical, cultural, psychological assumptions, the consistency of his tastes, values, memories, brings to a crisis his relation with language.—Barthes]<br /><br />Rhapsodic painting and music. Presaged by Romantics Coleridge, Wordsworth, Liszt. An imagination intensified by anxiety. Decay. (Presaged themselves by Romeo, Macbeth, Hamlet?) The mad stoned suicidally romantic artist. Looking ahead to the Beats, to beat daddy Kerouac! Wild rhapsodic self-destruction. <br /><br /> “We painted and read together, or I listened, as if in a dream, to the wild improvisations of his speaking guitar.” <br /><br />“A small picture [made by Roderick] presented the interior of an immensely long and rectangular vault or tunnel, with low walls, smooth, white, and without interruption or device.” <br /><br />“He not infrequently accompanied himself with rhymed verbal improvisations.” [Hey! Flash forward to rap! Spoken word?]<br /><br />The narrator and Usher seem to speak little, and alternately Usher lapsed into melancholy, or wild incoherent, rhapsodic talking. <br /><br />“Manic depression. . . ”—Jimi Hendrix<br /><br /> “A mere nervous affection, he [Roderick] immediately added, which would undoubtedly soon pass off” (as Lady Macbeth claimed about Macbeth who freaks out at the sight of Banquo’s ghost).<br />The house is, as if it were, his very soul, weighing on him. In contemporary gothic tales, Sylvie and Ruth and Lucie in Housekeeping, and We Have Always Lived in the Castle, the gothic House is the Soul. And the House seen as a tomb.<br /><br />So Madeline dies, put in a vault in the musty room below our narrator. <br />In grief, agitation, driven to madness, Usher succumbs to “gazing upon vacancy for long hours.” <br /><br />They write, they draw, they play music, but they also read books, too, to heal, or to further sink into the gloom: the literature of dread. Life echoes the story the narrator is reading.<br /><br />A knocking. What? Who’s there?! But she’s dead! Buried? Buried Madeline alive?! <br /><br />“Madman! I tell you that she now stands without the door!” [Shudder]<br /><br />(As I teenager I saw a B Vincent Price movie, “Premature Burial,” at a drive-in, and I actually screamed from the Big Reveal: SHE WAS ALIVE! SHE WAS ALIVE! SHE WAS ALIVE!)<br /><br />Wild storm, house cracks at the fissure we early learned about, collapses (no spoiler here, remember that title) into the tarn [a small mountain lake!]. Nature in all its voluptuousness takes the house back into itself. <br /><br />So. I liked it. There’s too little dialogue in the story, which for me is a fault, but it has its moments. A classic gothic horror story!</span>
  <a data-text-id="5206957705232947831" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1885790385" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1885790385" />
    <a title="A. Dawes" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/55066255-a-dawes"><img alt="A. Dawes" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1470731545p2/55066255.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1885790385?book_show_action=true">Jan 21, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="A. Dawes" class="user" itemprop="url" name="A. Dawes" href="/user/show/55066255-a-dawes">A. Dawes</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1885790385" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10208902508669527248">4.5*<br/><br/>Although this novelette was published in 1839, Poe is not the founder of the gothic horror genre, in fact Ann Radcliffe's The Mysteries of Udolpho was published a good deal earlier - and thankfully is enjoying a mini-revival of late. <br/><br/>But "The Fall of the House of Usher" could be seen as a successful model for novelette length gothic-horror works. This is relatively slow-paced when compared to modern day Seanan McGuire stories or Ellen Datlow anthologies, but it still had me utterly gripped. </span>
  <span id="freeText10208902508669527248" style="display:none">4.5*<br /><br />Although this novelette was published in 1839, Poe is not the founder of the gothic horror genre, in fact Ann Radcliffe's The Mysteries of Udolpho was published a good deal earlier - and thankfully is enjoying a mini-revival of late. <br /><br />But "The Fall of the House of Usher" could be seen as a successful model for novelette length gothic-horror works. This is relatively slow-paced when compared to modern day Seanan McGuire stories or Ellen Datlow anthologies, but it still had me utterly gripped. Poe uses gothic elements in detail, and the insight here into both place and character, make for a compellingly chilly atmosphere. Poe is also surprisingly poetic with his wonderfully descriptive imagery.<br /><br />This is not only a forerunner to short gothic horror, it also certainly still holds its place today as a great read.</span>
  <a data-text-id="10208902508669527248" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Kindle Edition " href="/book/show/18889045-the-fall-of-the-house-of-usher">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/37374526-classic-reverie?shelf=edgar-allan-poe">edgar-allan-poe</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/37374526-classic-reverie?shelf=old-time-radio-reference">old-time-radio-reference</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/37374526-classic-reverie?shelf=horror-classic">horror-classic</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/37374526-classic-reverie?shelf=1800">1800</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/37374526-classic-reverie?shelf=short-stories">short-stories</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1792583957" class="readable"
              >

<div id="review_1442758666" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1442758666" />
    <a title="Sara" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5512657-sara"><img alt="Sara" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1543780395p2/5512657.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1442758666?book_show_action=true">Jun 04, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Sara" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Sara" href="/user/show/5512657-sara">Sara</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>


          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">lovers or Poe, horror, or Boris Karloff</span></div>

        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5512657-sara?shelf=catching-up-classics">catching-up-classics</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5512657-sara?shelf=american-classics">american-classics</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5512657-sara?shelf=short-stories-novellas">short-stories-novellas</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5512657-sara?shelf=kindle-purchase">kindle-purchase</a>,

          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1442758666" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13950734444069836117">Oh come on, how is this not fun. Read on a dark night, one when the lights are out because there is a furious storm beating on your rooftop and windows, it would make you shudder indeed.<br/><br/>It is not my first reading, but it might be my most appreciative one. I reveled in the description, the careful choice of words, the building agitation of our narrator. I picked up on one tidbit I might have missed before. Very early on in the narrator's description of Roderick Usher (who doesn't love that name?)</span>
  <span id="freeText13950734444069836117" style="display:none">Oh come on, how is this not fun. Read on a dark night, one when the lights are out because there is a furious storm beating on your rooftop and windows, it would make you shudder indeed.<br /><br />It is not my first reading, but it might be my most appreciative one. I reveled in the description, the careful choice of words, the building agitation of our narrator. I picked up on one tidbit I might have missed before. Very early on in the narrator's description of Roderick Usher (who doesn't love that name?), we are told his <i>"family had been noted, time our of mind, for a peculiar sensibility of temperament, displaying itself, through long ages, in many works of exalted art..."</i> As an artist who seriously teetered on the edge of madness himself, I wonder how completely Poe connected art and insanity; how much he feared that the very sensitive and artist personality might succumb to it.<br /><br />Having just finished a historical (biographical) novel of Poe, <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/16130398.Mrs__Poe" title="Mrs. Poe by Lynn Cullen" rel="nofollow noopener">Mrs. Poe</a>, I had an itch to revisit some of his tales. I was tickled that this one was picked for a group read. Now, off to see what others are saying about it.</span>
  <a data-text-id="13950734444069836117" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">

        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

              <span id="reviewTextContainer1473305294" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4226305728526976018">That. Was. Fantastic.<br/><br/>It's been so long since I've read Poe, or anything like Poe, and that was just so refreshing. It was so great to be confronted with a story that presented a challenge in so many ways: the diction, the sub-meanings, the sheer horror of the plot! After such a long YA binge, this was just wonderful and exactly what I needed to get out of my reading slump.<br/><br/>I love everything about Poe's writing. The long and winding sentences that make you sit back and think, but also grip you wit</span>
  <span id="freeText4226305728526976018" style="display:none">That. Was. Fantastic.<br /><br />It's been so long since I've read Poe, or anything like Poe, and that was just so refreshing. It was so great to be confronted with a story that presented a challenge in so many ways: the diction, the sub-meanings, the sheer horror of the plot! After such a long YA binge, this was just wonderful and exactly what I needed to get out of my reading slump.<br /><br />I love everything about Poe's writing. The long and winding sentences that make you sit back and think, but also grip you with just the sheer beauty and intricacies of their crafting, just, ahh. Wonderful. And I'd forgotten just how masterful he was at writings true suspense and horror! The deliberate and detailed creation of the setting and atmosphere just took my breath away. I had goosebumps the entire time.<br /><br />Now that I've been reminded how much I love Poe, I can feel a marathon coming on!</span>
  <a data-text-id="4226305728526976018" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_3240609447" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3240609447" />
    <a title="Scarlett Readz and Runz....Through Novel Time &amp; Distance" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/53419883-scarlett-readz-and-runz-through-novel-time-distance"><img alt="Scarlett Readz and Runz....Through Novel Time &amp; Distance" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1600345081p2/53419883.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3240609447?book_show_action=true">Apr 04, 2020</a>

<div id="review_2878203659" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2878203659" />
    <a title="Martha " class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/7212128-martha"><img alt="Martha " src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1555330492p2/7212128.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2878203659?book_show_action=true">Nov 01, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Martha " class="user" itemprop="url" name="Martha " href="/user/show/7212128-martha">Martha </a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7212128-martha?shelf=own-books">own-books</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2878203659" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2910326159378158661"><br/>“Son coeur est un luth suspendu; dès que nous le touchons, il résonne.”<br/>Pierre-Jean de Béranger<br/><br/>“His heart is a suspended lute; as soon as we touch it, it sounds.”<br/><br/>Ah, the prose of Edgar Allan Poe. <br/><br/>Mr. Poe - Mr. Ghost-Writer himself:<br/><br/>“Mad Trist” of Sir Launcelot Canning<br/><br/>“The Haunted Palace”<br/><br/>Within the hustle and bustle of our everyday lives- along comes Edgar Allan Poe. This is not an author you skim read. Oh, no. You read. Stop. Think. Look up words you have never seen before in your life; stop - rum</span>
  <span id="freeText2910326159378158661" style="display:none"><br />“Son coeur est un luth suspendu; dès que nous le touchons, il résonne.”<br />Pierre-Jean de Béranger<br /><br />“His heart is a suspended lute; as soon as we touch it, it sounds.”<br /><br />Ah, the prose of Edgar Allan Poe. <br /><br />Mr. Poe - Mr. Ghost-Writer himself:<br /><br />“Mad Trist” of Sir Launcelot Canning<br /><br />“The Haunted Palace”<br /><br />Within the hustle and bustle of our everyday lives- along comes Edgar Allan Poe. This is not an author you skim read. Oh, no. You read. Stop. Think. Look up words you have never seen before in your life; stop - ruminate the words. Yes, this is an author you plan to sit with for a while and really absorb. Great stuff.<br /><br />Such prose as to have a dictionary at my elbow; the spark notes at my wrist; and the closing of my eyes to think in an upright position (which turned into my noggin napping which in turn caused my neck a snapping -ouch).<br /><br />I love to be challenged by excellent writing and by gosh the descriptions within the first two pages of <i>The Fall of the House of Usher</i> have been undefinably challenging. <br /><br />If you do not feel the manse’s cold, empty, oppressive eeriness by the end of the second page, you definitely were not paying attention here. <br /><br />The paragraph below, within <i>The Fall of the House of Usher</i>, is one of many so beautiful.<br /><br />“I reined my horse to the precipitous brink of a black and lurid tarn that lay in unruffled lustre by the dwelling, and gazed down — but with a shudder even more thrilling than before — upon the remodelled and inverted images of the gray sedge, and ghastly tree-stems, and the vacant and eye-like windows.” <br /><br />Yes, “eye-like” and “upon the vacant eye-like windows” referenced throughout. Oh, the Orbs. The manse is alive!<br /><br />And, that darn “tarn”- my goodness it is referenced a few times. Everything comes together in the end.<br /><br />And, how about the phantasmic story of Ethelred which worked in sync with the events of the evening. “With blows, made quickly room in the plankings of the door for his gauntleted hand; and now pulling therewith sturdily, he so cracked, and ripped, and tore all asunder, that the noise of the dry and hollow-sounding wood alarmed and reverberated throughout the forest.” <br /><br />Aha! So cleverly slipped into the main story of the Usher household. <br /><br />Absolutely loved this story!</span>
  <a data-text-id="2910326159378158661" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <span id="freeText16538902965673567835" style="display:none">Read again, June 8, 2017:<br /><br />When I read the last word I exhaled a breath of awe and felt like a child who had ridden his first roller coaster -- "Woe! That was so freaking awesome. Oh my God!" I said this with whispering, quivering excitement. I read this in elementary school but just now, at 39, understood the connection of the story, the slaying of the "dragon," the power of this house to turn fantasy into reality. Poe relies heavily on setting, a Gothic masterpiece in the beginning, which has a slow start. The last few pages stirred me with the whirlwind, the pace, the awe, the excitement!<br /><br />I begin to understand why a crazy fan of Poe's sneaks into his graveyard on his birthday every year, dressed and hidden in black, and places a flower on his grave in the still, cold night.<br /><br />------------------------------<br />2015: My first time. So awesome. I may become a fan, so impressed, like watching a movie .</span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1675680592" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4478376343179301431">This is the first of Poe's stories I've read. I came across an astonishing reading, as so many of my friends have early described. <br/><br/>Poe's style shows us how every element of a short story is meaningful. I've recently read a meta-linguistic narrative in which the author said "No useless words, all of them, the absolute totality, loaded with signification. Novel readers have time to lose; short-stories readers, don't." While reading this book, I couldn't agree more. <br/><br/>I adored the gothic style, just </span>
  <span id="freeText4478376343179301431" style="display:none">This is the first of Poe's stories I've read. I came across an astonishing reading, as so many of my friends have early described. <br /><br />Poe's style shows us how every element of a short story is meaningful. I've recently read a meta-linguistic narrative in which the author said "No useless words, all of them, the absolute totality, loaded with signification. Novel readers have time to lose; short-stories readers, don't." While reading this book, I couldn't agree more. <br /><br />I adored the gothic style, just as the guilt, disgrace and fear carried by Roderick Usher, one of the main characters of this narrative. His hyperesthesia and hypochondria really potentialized the horror in the story, just as Poe's brilliant style. I'm really looking forward to more of Edgar Allan Poe's work. </span>
  <a data-text-id="4478376343179301431" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

  <span id="freeText16650864770667175925" style="display:none">Wow, that was good! I don't think I've read this one before, although I have definitely heard of it. I connected it for two reasons--one, being in social isolation, I, an extrovert, am finding ways to keep myself busy and sane. I can imagine if I were isolated in a place like the House of Usher, I would easily go insane. But the other thing is that besides all the online tutoring I've been doing (thank God), I've also been doing a lot of reading as a way to distract myself. I also have been taking solo walks and listening to books during that time. I've been very fortunate that I've found some great books to distract myself with, but listening to all the titles mentioned in this short story, I'm not surprised they fed into his insanity, lol. I both listened to and read this book and I thought the narrator did an excellent job!</span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1303495759" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17456899970736918512">Ugh. I just love Poe's stories so much. Good thing he wrote many, so I still have more to go.<br/><br/><img src='https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1433888643i/15154560.jpg' class='gr-hostedUserImg'/><br/><br/>The narrator goes to Roderick Usher's house because he's summoned due to his friend's illness. There, he starts to notice some strange things, some of which include Usher's sister weird behaviour and subsequent death.<br/><br/>Even when this was so short, I found Usher to be an intriguing character. Utterly creepy but really interesting. His condition of hypochondria and axiety made him even more intriguing, and a</span>
  <span id="freeText17456899970736918512" style="display:none">Ugh. I just love Poe's stories so much. Good thing he wrote many, so I still have more to go.<br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1433888643i/15154560.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>The narrator goes to Roderick Usher's house because he's summoned due to his friend's illness. There, he starts to notice some strange things, some of which include Usher's sister weird behaviour and subsequent death.<br><br>Even when this was so short, I found Usher to be an intriguing character. Utterly creepy but really interesting. His condition of hypochondria and axiety made him even more intriguing, and also, it was crucial to the plot.<br><br>And the ending... damn. I loved it, but damn. Poe sure knew how to write horror, and I love that.<br><br><b>Why should you read it?</b><br><br>· Gothic house: <img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1413989258i/11590864._SY540_.png" height="15" width='15"/' class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>· Horror and suspense: <img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1413989258i/11590864._SY540_.png" height="15" width='15"/' class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>· Keeps you thrilled until the end: <img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1413989258i/11590864._SY540_.png" height="15" width='15"/' class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>· Writing that provokes chills down your spine: <img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1413989258i/11590864._SY540_.png" height="15" width='15"/' class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>· Written by Poe: <img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1413989258i/11590864._SY540_.png" height="15" width='15"/' class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>Overall, <b>it met all my expectations.</b> It's an insane good book.<br><br>P.S.: I love Poe.</span>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('gfiKK+W9e5VUSiBMQpJ746WpYKoVJLtqhl5VijbS4BHEDkRz9QDuzQRKJjnBGvfW89K9Eji3BXLOOtgmWqDXOA==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('U6682U6LtPeIjkTyLEgGUgm4126UQEKPTDw8aCMQA9IWWHKBXjYhr9iOQoevwIpnX8MK1rnT/JcEWLHET2I0+w==')})
  }

Gertrude Franklin Horn Atherton (October 30, 1857 - June 14, 1948) was a prominent and prolific American author, many of whose novels are based in her home state, California. Her best-seller Black Oxen (1923) was made into a silent movie of the same name. In addition to novels, she wrote short stories, essays, and articles for magazines and newspapers on such issues as feminism, politics, and war. She was strong-willed, independent-minded, and sometimes controversial. Atherton\'s first publication was &quot;The Randolphs of Redwood: A Romance,&quot; serialized in The Argonaut in March 1882 under the pseudonym Asmodeus. When she revealed to her family that she was the author, it caused her to be ostracized. In 1888, she left for New York, leaving Muriel with her grandmother. She traveled to London, and eventually returned to California. Atherton\'s first novel, What Dreams May Come, was published in 1888 under the pseudonym Frank Lin. In 1889, she went to Paris at the invitation of her sister-in-law Alejandra Rathbone (married to Major Jared Lawrence Rathbone). That year, she heard from British publisher G. Routledge and Sons that they would publish her first two books. William Sharp wrote in The Spectator praising her fiction and would later invite Atherton to stay with him and his wife, Elizabeth, in South Hampstead. In London, she had the opportunity through Jane Wilde to meet Oscar Wilde, her son. She recalled in her memoir Adventures of a Novelist (1932) that she made an excuse to avoid the meeting because she thought he was physically repulsive.       <div id="aboutAuthor" class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/author/show/4624490.Edgar_Allan_Poe">About Edgar Allan Poe</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
          <div class='bookAuthorProfile'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__topContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__photoContainer'>
<a href="/author/show/4624490.Edgar_Allan_Poe"><div class='bookAuthorProfile__photo' style='background-image: url(https://images.gr-assets.com/authors/1454522972p3/4624490.jpg);'></div>
</a></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__widgetContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__name'>
<a href="/author/show/4624490.Edgar_Allan_Poe">Edgar Allan Poe
</a></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__followerCount'>
20,391
followers
</div>

</div>
</div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer13946967777536569794">The name Poe brings to mind images of murderers and madmen, premature burials, and mysterious women who return from the dead. His works have been in print since 1827 and include such literary classics as <i>The Tell-Tale Heart</i>, <i>The Raven</i>, and The Fall of the House of Usher. This versatile writer’s oeuvre includes short stories, poetry, a novel, a textbook, a book of scientific theory, and hundreds of</span>
  <span id="freeText13946967777536569794" style="display:none">The name Poe brings to mind images of murderers and madmen, premature burials, and mysterious women who return from the dead. His works have been in print since 1827 and include such literary classics as <i>The Tell-Tale Heart</i>, <i>The Raven</i>, and The Fall of the House of Usher. This versatile writer’s oeuvre includes short stories, poetry, a novel, a textbook, a book of scientific theory, and hundreds of essays and book reviews. He is widely acknowledged as the inventor of the modern detective story and an innovator in the science fiction genre, but he made his living as America’s first great literary critic and theoretician. Poe’s reputation today rests primarily on his tales of terror as well as on his haunting lyric poetry.<br /><br />Just as the bizarre characters in Poe’s stories have captured the public imagination so too has Poe himself. He is seen as a morbid, mysterious figure lurking in the shadows of moonlit cemeteries or crumbling castles. This is the Poe of legend. But much of what we know about Poe is wrong, the product of a biography written by one of his enemies in an attempt to defame the author’s name.<br /><br />The real Poe was born to traveling actors in Boston on January 19, 1809. Edgar was the second of three children. His other brother William Henry Leonard Poe would also become a poet before his early death, and Poe’s sister Rosalie Poe would grow up to teach penmanship at a Richmond girls’ school. Within three years of Poe’s birth both of his parents had died, and he was taken in by the wealthy tobacco merchant John Allan and his wife Frances Valentine Allan in Richmond, Virginia while Poe’s siblings went to live with other families. Mr. Allan would rear Poe to be a businessman and a Virginia gentleman, but Poe had dreams of being a writer in emulation of his childhood hero the British poet Lord Byron. Early poetic verses found written in a young Poe’s handwriting on the backs of Allan’s ledger sheets reveal how little interest Poe had in the tobacco business.<br /><br />For more information, please see <a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Edgar_allan_poe">http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Edgar_al...</a></span>
  <a data-text-id="13946967777536569794" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

</div>
